  \item \textbf{Autoregressive image generation:} The idea is that we first convert an image into a sequence of discrete visual tokens, and then we train a transformer to predict those tokens one by one.
  This is very similar to LLM, because it is still next-token prediction, just over visual tokens instead of words.

  \paragraph{Typical Recipe:} (1) Train an image tokenizer, often with something like VQ-VAE / VQGAN. (2) Represent each image as a grid/sequence of codebook indices. (3) Train a transformer conditioned on text to autoregressively predict these indices. (4) Decode the indices back to pixels.

  Typically, we have long token sequences for images, and the sampling is slow. Historically, it results in weaker image quality than strong diffusion systems.

  \item \textbf{Diffusion / flow-style generation:} This is the dominant idea for high-quality image generation\footnote{More precisely, today it is usually diffusion or a very close relative such as flow matching / rectified-flow-style models.}.
  The idea is that instead of predicting image tokens one by one, we ask the model to learns to iteratively denoise a noisy image latent.
\end{itemize}


Top level facts: (1) Diffusion learns a reverse stochastic process from noise to data. (2) Flow matching / rectified flow learns a deterministic ODE flow from noise to data.

Let $p_{\text{data}}$ be the target data distribution on $\R^d$, for images $d = HWC$ in pixel space, or much more commonly in practice, $d$ is the dimension of a learned latent code\footnote{Latent diffusion models explicitly move the diffusion process into latent space for efficiency, and use cross-attention to inject text conditioning.}.

\paragraph{Diffusion Models}
The core idea is 
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Define a forward process that gradually destroys structure by adding noise.
  \item Learn a reverse process that gradually removes the noise.
  \item To generate a sample, start from pure noise and run the learned reverse process.
\end{enumerate}
So baically, the diffusion models turn the hard problem of sample $x_0 \sim p_{\text{data}}$ into many easier local denoising problems.

\paragraph{The forward diffusion process}
The forward process is fixed, at each step, we add a small amount of Gaussian noise to the data sample (we will use $x_0$ to denote the initial clean data sample).
\begin{align*}
  q(x_t \mid x_{t - 1}) = \cN (x_t; \sqrt{\alpha_t} x_{t-1}, \beta_t \mI_d),
\end{align*}
We are taking the mean as a scaled version of the previous state, $\alpha_t = 1 - \beta_t$, this gradually destroys information over time. 
$\beta_t$ controls the amount of noise added.
This is equivalent to the sampling equation 
\begin{align*}
  x_t = \sqrt{\alpha_t} x_{t - 1} + \sqrt{\beta_t}\epsilon, \quad \epsilon \sim \cN(0, \mI_d).
\end{align*}
After many steps, the sample becomes almost pure Gaussion noise.
A very important fact is that we can write $x_t$ as a noisy version of $x_0$,
\begin{align*}
  q(x_t \mid x_0) = \cN (x_t; \sqrt{\bar{\alpha}_t}x_0, (1 - \bar{\alpha}_t)\mI_d).
\end{align*}
where $\bar{\alpha}_t$ is defined as 
\begin{align*}
  \bar{\alpha}_t \eqdef \prod_{s=1}^{t} \alpha_s.
\end{align*}
This matters because during it means that during training, we do not need to simulate the whole chain step by step. We can sample a random $t$ and construct $x_t$ directly from $x_0$.


\paragraph{The reverse process: the real generative model}
The reverse process is what we learn.
Let $x_{0:T}$ be the whole collection of $\cbrac{x_0, x_1, \hdots, x_T}$.
The diffusion model defines a joint distribution over the whole reverse trajectory:
\begin{align}
  \label{eq:denoising}
  p_{\theta}(x_{0:T}) = p(x_T)\cdot \prod_{t=1}^T p_{\theta}(x_{t-1} \mid x_t).
\end{align}
Here $\theta$ is the trainable parameter, $x_T$ is the starting point of the reverse process, which is standard Gaussian noise: 
\begin{align*}
  p(x_T) = \cN(x_T; 0, \mI_d).
\end{align*}
This means that before any denoising happens, the state $x_T$ is sampled from a standard normal distribution.
From (\ref{eq:denoising}), we know that once $x_T$ is sampled, we repeatedly sample $x_{t-1}$ from $p_{\theta}(x_{t-1}\mid x_t)$, as we are gradually denoising.
This formula is built upon the underlying Markov assumption.

Note that the true object we care about is the $x_0$, but not the whole chain.
So after defining the joint data distribution $p_{\theta}(x_{0:T})$, we obtain the model's distribution on clean samples by marginalizing out the intermediate states:
\begin{align*}
  p_{\theta}(x_0) = \int p_{\theta}(x_{0:T}) dx_1 \hdots dx_T.
\end{align*}
This basically means that in order to compute the probability of a clean sample $x_0$, we must account for all possible noisy trajectories that could have led to it.


\paragraph{How do we generate a sample in practice?}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Step 1: sample pure noise: $x_{T} \sim \cN(0, \mI)$, which gives a completely random Gaussian vector. 
  \item Step 2: denoise one step, and repeat: $x_{T-1} \sim p_{\theta}(x_{T - 1} \mid x_T)$, and repeat.
  \item Step 3: Output the final clean sample.
\end{enumerate}

One might ask, how do we actually parametrize $p_{\theta}(x_{t-1} \mid x_t)$?
A conditional probability distribution can be very complicated.
The standard choice is to assume $p_\theta(x_{t-1} \mid x_t)$ is Gaussian.


\textbf{Unfinished.}


\section{Miscellaneous}

\subsection{Chinchilla Optimality}
Chinchilla optimalilty refers to the balance between model size (number of parameters) and dataset size (number of training tokens) for a fixed budget of compute.
\begin{itemize}
  \item Before Chinchilla, the common practice (e.g. GPT-3) was to train very large models on relatively small datasets. This made the model undertrained, which means that they had more parameters than the dataset could properly fit.
  \item The Chinchilla paper by Deepmind in 2022 systematically showed that for a fixed budget of compute, the optimal stragety is not to make the model as large as possible, but instead to use a smaller model and train it on much more data.
  \item Roughly speaking, they found that the optimal scaling law would be letting the number of training tokens scale linearly with the number of parameters
  \item The empirical results show that the optimal data-to-model ratio to be around 20 tokens per paramete ($1\times$ case)\footnote{We often see $2\times$ or $4\times$ cases where we increase the number of tokens $2$ or $4$ times.}.
  If we train with fewer tokens per parameter such as $4 \sim 5$ tokens per parameter, the model would be under-trained, and if we train with more tokens per parameter such as $50 \sim 100$ tokens per parameter, the model would be over-trained.
\end{itemize}

In practice, GPT-3 had 175B parameters but was trained on 300B tokens.
Chinchilla showed that 70B parameters trained on 1.4T tokens (with the same compute budget) achieves much better performance.


\subsection{Metrics (nats, bits, bpb)}
The standard language modeling loss is the average of the negative log likelihood of the next token.
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{LM}} = -\frac{1}{L}\sum_{t=1}^{L}\log\probsub{\theta}{\mx_t \mid \mx_{< t}},
\end{align*}
where $\mx = (\mx_1, \hdots, \mx_L)$ is a sequence of tokens.
The loss it self can be reported in nats (natural log base $e$) $L_{\text{nats}}$ or bits (log base $2$) $L_{\text{bits}}$.
If we exponentiate the average loss, we get perplexity.

There is another related measure, namely bits per bytes (bpb), coming from viewing the model as a compression system, which connects to Shannon's source coding theorem.
That is, the average number of bits needed to encode a symbol is equal to its entropy (expected negative log likelihood).
Under this view, the bits $L_{\text{bits}}$ indicates how many bits are needed to encode each token.
This already gives us bits per token.
However, tokens are not uniform units, because they vary in length, so bits per token is not directly comparable across tokenization schemes.
This is where bpb comes in.
Instead of normalizing by token count, normalize by raw UTF-8 byte count of the text.
If we denote the dataset text length as $T$-tokens and $B$-bytes, then 
\begin{align*}
  \text{bpb} = \frac{T}{B}\cdot L_{\text{bits}}.
\end{align*}
This makes the result more stable and comparable across models with different tokenizer.


\subsection{Model Distillation}
\textbf{Model distillation (knowledge distillation)} is the process where a large ``teacher'' model trains a smaller ``student'' model to imitate its behavior.
The student learns from:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the teacher's soft logits
  \item intermediate features
  \item responses / reasoning traces
  \item tool usage / actions
  \item synthetic data generated by the teacher
\end{itemize}
The concept of distillation is now new, but recently it becomes popular because of LLMs.
It can be used to perform cost reduction since we can distill a 70B or 405B model into a 3B - 8B model that retains ~ 90\% performance.
Also the technique can be applied for fast inferencing.

We can also add safety + controllability, where we distill ``desired behaviors'' and filter out bad outputs.
This is frequently done after RLHF or safety tuning.
Llama 3.1-8B, Qwen2.5-7B, Mistral-7B are all extremely good because they were trained with teacher signals from larger ``pre-RLHF'' and ``post-RLHF'' teachers.


\paragraph{Logit distillation:} 
This is the classical way, where the student tries to match the teacher logits on tokens.
It is a strong signal, but requires access to teacher logits, but teacher is often closed models and will not provide such information.

If we think about it, it is nothing more than forcing the student to imitate the teacher's probability distribution over the vocabulary at every training step.

Let us imagine we have an input batch of token ids $\mx \in \R^{B \times L}$, we can first run the forward pass of the teacher and obtain teacher hidden state $\mH^{T} \in \R^{B \times L \times d}$, and the teacher logits $\mZ^{T} \in \R^{B \times L \times V}$.
\begin{lstlisting}
  with torch.no_grad():
    teacher_logits = teacher(x).logits       # shape [B, L, V]
\end{lstlisting}
For temperature $T > 0$, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \mP^T = \softmax(\frac{\mZ^T}{T}).
\end{align*}
\begin{lstlisting}
  teacher_probs = softmax(teacher_logits / temperature, dim=-1) # shape [B, L, V]
\end{lstlisting}
For student model, we can also run forward and obtain hidden state $\mH^S \in \R^{B \times L \times d^\prime}$ ($d^\prime \leq d$), and $\mZ^S \in \R^{B \times L \times V}$.
\begin{lstlisting}
  student_logits = student(x).logits   # shape [B, L, V]
  student_probs  = softmax(student_logits / temperature, dim=-1)
\end{lstlisting}

\paragraph{Distillation loss via KL divergence.}
Then we define the distillation loss: 
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{distill}} = T^2 \cdot \klD{\mP^T}{\mP^S}.
\end{align*}
\begin{lstlisting}
  log_student = log_softmax(student_logits / temperature, dim=-1)
  loss_distill = (temperature**2) * torch.nn.functional.kl_div(
      log_student, teacher_probs, reduction="batchmean"
  )
\end{lstlisting}
Note that 
\begin{align*}
  \klD{P}{Q} = \sum_{i}P_i \rbrac{\log P_i - \log Q_i}.
\end{align*}

\paragraph{Optional Cross-Entropy loss.}
If the label $\my \in \R^{B \times L}$ exists, then  
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{CE}} = -\frac{1}{BL}\sum_{b, l} \log \mP^S(y_{b, l})
\end{align*}
\begin{lstlisting}
  loss_ce = cross_entropy(
      student_logits.view(B*L, V),
      y.view(B*L),
    )
\end{lstlisting}
Then we can take the combination of the two losses.


\paragraph{Response-style distillation (instruction tuning).}
This is the modern workhorse and there is no teacher's logits required.
We only use the response generated by the teacher.
In this case, each sample is consisted of the instruction $\mx \in \R^{L_x}$, and the response generated by the teacher $\my^t \in \R^{L_y}$,  the student then perform standard supervised learning on the input batched input consisted of $\mx$ and $\my^t$, and we will only compute the CE loss on the teacher generated part.

\begin{lstlisting}
  logits = student(input_ids)                     # [B, Lx+Ly, V]
  loss = cross_entropy(
      logits[:, shift: , :].reshape(-1, V),
      y_teach_tokens.reshape(-1),
  )
\end{lstlisting}
This is done in models such as LLaMA-3 Instruct, Qwen2.5-Instruct and etc.
It is easy to see why this works, since teacher's generated text encodes reasoning trajectory, style, task formatting, chain-of-thought (if included), tool-use patterns and how to structure answers.
The student simply learns the distribution of responses.
This is cheaper but weaker than logit distillation
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Pros:} (i) works with closed models; (ii) much easier and cheaper; (iii) easy dataset scaling; (iv) captures high-level style and reasoning patterns.
  \item \textbf{Cons:} (i) student never sees teacher's full token distribution; (ii) sensitive to sampling biases from teacher and cannot fully preserve teacher's performance ceiling.
\end{itemize}


\paragraph{Reasoning Distillation (CoT / ReAct / Reflection).}
Student learns step-by-step reasoning patterns generated by a stronger teacher.
These methods do not aim to imitate final answers, instead they teach the internal structure of reasoning.

\paragraph{Synthetic data distillation.}
The core idea is that instead of using a real human dataset, we ask a strong teacher model to invent the dataset itself.
The student then trains on this synthetic dataset.
The technique has become one of the most powerful techniques for frontier models.

Take math for example:
\begin{itemize}
  \item We first decide topic / difficulty / format (GSM8K-style, MATH-style, AMC/AIME-style), which defines the task distribution we want the teacher to generate.
  
  \item Then, we prepare a meta-prompt for the teacher: 
  \begin{verbatim}
    Generate a new math problem
    Then generate a correct solution
    Then produce the final numeric or symbolic answer
    Follow a consistent format
    Avoid trivial rephrasings of known datasets
    Ensure the problem is solvable
  \end{verbatim}
  This fully controls the structure of the synthetic sample.

  \item We then ask the teacher to generate a new math problem, then immediately follow a solution, which is then combined into a training example.
  
  \item Verification pass is needed, typically, a separate or the same teacher instance or verifier model is asked to check if the reasoning is correct and if the final answer matches the reasoning. 
  Based on whether it is correct or not, we decide to keep or regenerate the response.
  This ensures the quality of the dataset.
  
  \item We may also want to do deduplication and filtering, which filters out near-duplicate problems, structurally similar templates, formatting problem or ill-posed problem and invalid numeric parameters.
  This ensures dataset diversity and correctness.

  \item Curriculum tuning: we often organize the synthetic data in a way that they become increasingly difficult.
\end{itemize}


\paragraph{Training \& Evaluations:}

\begin{itemize}
  \item Math: 
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Training:} GSM8K-synthetic expansions, MATH, MathQA.
    \item \textbf{Evaluations:} GSM8K, MATH, MATH500 (Minerva subset), AMC 10/12, AIME, OlympiadBench.
  \end{itemize}

  \item Code:
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Training:} The Stack v1/v2, GitHub repositories (filtered), CodeSearchNet.
    \item \textbf{Evaluations:} HumanEval, MBPP, APPS (Intro / Inter / Hard)
  \end{itemize}

  \item Logic and Generic Reasoning:
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Training:} Synthetic CoT datasets (massive), Self-generated reasoning traces (DeepSeek-R1 style), GSM8K-style CoT explanations.
    \item \textbf{Evaluations:} BBH (Big Bench Hard), StrategyQA, CommonsenseQA.
  \end{itemize}

  \item Other aspects include: Multi-hop \& knowledge QA, instruction following (general SFT), Tool Use \& Function Calling, Safety and alignment. 
  
  \item There are also certain meta benchmarks such as MMLU (57 subjects; ``IQ-test'' for LLMs), MMLU-Pro (harder reasoning + domain knowledge), HELM (holistic evaluation).
\end{itemize}


\paragraph{Feature Distillation}
Student imitates internal representations of the teacher, not just logits or final outputs.
This happens when we want a smaller model to behave like a larger one and we want better generalization than response distillation alone.
It is commonly used in tiny transformers and quantized models.


\subsection{Model Compression}
Modern LLMs (7B to 405B) are expensive to run. Inference cost is dominated by memory bandwidth (moving weights around), activation sizes, KV cache growth in long-context inference.
In general there are 4 types of model compression, which we have seen most of them.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Quantization:} Convert high precision weights/activations into low precision. This includes 
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Weight-only quantization:} W4A16, W3A16 and W2A16. This is practical for most LLMs, and preserve accuracy superisingly well.
    \item \textbf{Activation quantization:} Needed for fully low-precision inference.
    \item \textbf{KV-cache quantization:} Large gains for long-context inference.
    \item \textbf{Group quantization:} reduces precision but protects outliers. (weight vector splitted further into small-sized groups.)
  \end{itemize}

  \item \textbf{Pruning:} Pruning removes unnecessary weights or even the entire neurons.
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Unstructured pruning:} Remove individual weights based on magnitude. Hard to accelerate on GPU.
    \item \textbf{Structured pruning:} Remove entire attention heads, MLP channels, blocks, leads to real speedups.
  \end{itemize}
  This will be discussed in detail later.

  \item \textbf{Distillaton:} As discussed previously, teacher and student framework.
  
  \item \textbf{Low-rank factorization:}
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Training time compression:} LoRA reduces trainable parameters.
    \item \textbf{Inference time compression:} An example would be we have a big matrix $\mW$, we can compute an SVD or similar factorization offline decomposing it into $\mW \simeq \mU_r\mathbf{\Sigma}_r\mV_r = \mA\mB$, and we store only $\mA, \mB$ or compute $\mA(\mB x)$ when inferencing, which reduces memory footprint or compute.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}


\subsection{Pruning}
The general idea would be to remove weights, neurons or even the entire attention heads / MLP blocks while trying to preserve accuracy.
This helps reduce memory footprint, inference latency, compute cost.
Typically, there are two types of pruning.

\paragraph{Unstructured Pruning (weight pruning):}
Removes individual weights based on magnitude or other criteria.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Pros:} We could achieve high compression rate, and it is flexible.
  \item \textbf{Cons:} This creates irregular sparsity, which is not easy to use by hardwares, real speed ups require specialized sparse-kernel implementations.
\end{itemize}

This is still used in some places, but not an ideal technique for real development.

\paragraph{Structured Pruning:}
This is where most practical LLM pruning happens.
We remove groups of weights: such as the entire neurons in MLP, the entire attention head, the entire FFN channels. 
Sometimes an rare case is that we remove the entire layer.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Pros:} Gives actual speedup on standard hardware, easy to implement, and plays nicely with quantization and distilation.
\end{itemize}

Modern structured pruning methods include: Wanda, SparseGPT, LLaMA-Pro block pruning, outlier-aware pruning and GPTQ-aware channel pruning.


\paragraph{Activation / dynamic pruning:}
These methods disable computation for tokens or layers depending on runtime signals.
This is more like runtime optimization rather than classical pruning.


\paragraph{Specific Pruning Algorithms:}
Here, we review some of the pruning methods.

\textbullet~ \textbf{Simple Magnitude Pruning:} It could be (i) Compute the magnitude of weights $\lvert \mW_{ij} \rvert$ and drop the smallest. (ii) For structured pruning, sum over groups $g_k = \norm{\mW_k}_2$ and remove groups with the smallest norms.


\textbullet~ \textbf{SparseGPT:} (Mostly niche now.) post-training, weight-pruning method designed for LLM, it prunes billions of weights without retraining, while keeping perplexity degradation small.

Pruning is done layer-by-layer, block-by-block by solving a local quadratic minimization problem.
The \textbf{core motivation} here would be that we know pruning must change the weights, but we must not let the change of weights change the layer's output distribution.
Otherwise the error will propagates through dozen's of layers and the whole model collapses.

To keep the layer output almost unchanged, we need a way to predict how zeroing some weights will change the output.
Let us take an linear layer for example $\my = \mW \mx$.
Assume that we apply pruning to $\Delta \mW$ to $\mW \in \R^{m \times d}$, i.e., 
\begin{align*}
  \mW^\prime = \mW + \Delta \mW.
\end{align*}
The output will now become 
\begin{align*}
  \cL(\Delta \mW) = \my^\prime = \rbrac{\mW + \Delta \mW}\mx = \my + \Delta \mW \mx.
\end{align*}
We would like to have $\my^\prime \simeq \my$, so we minimize 
\begin{align*}
  \min_{\Delta \mW} = \ExpSub{\mx}{\norm{\Delta \mW x}^2}.
\end{align*}
Suppose that we have a calibration batch of $N$ activation vectors $\mX \in \R^{d \times N}$, each column corresponds to each sample.
We approximate the expectation via empirical mean:
\begin{align*}
  \cL(\Delta \mW) \simeq \frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^{N} \norm{\Delta \mW \mx_i} &= \frac{1}{N}\norm{\Delta \mW \mX}^2_{F} \\
  &= \frac{1}{N}\tr{\mX^{\top} (\Delta\mW)^{\top} \Delta \mW \mX} \\
  &= \tr{\Delta \mW \rbrac{\frac{1}{N}\mX\mX^{\top}}(\Delta \mW)^{\top}}.
\end{align*}
Define the empirical input variance $\mC = \frac{1}{N}\mX\mX^{\top} \in \R^{d \times d}$, we can write $\cL_{\Delta\mW} = \tr{\Delta \mW \mC (\Delta \mW)^{\top}}$.
Now, if we write $\delta = \vecc{\Delta \mW}$, we can rewrite the loss function using the trace vec identity as
\begin{align*}
  \cL(\delta) = \delta^{\top}\rbrac{\mC \otimes \mI_m} \delta.
\end{align*}
Denote $\omega = \vecc{\mW}$ and $\omega^\prime = \vecc{\mW^\prime}$, we have $\omega^\prime = \omega + \delta$, and since we are doing pruning, let $\cS$ be the set of indices of pruned coordinates, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \delta_i = \begin{cases}
    - \omega_i, \qquad &\text{if } i \in \cS, \\
    0, \qquad &\text{otherwise}.
  \end{cases}
\end{align*}
We can actually reorder the coordinates of $\delta$, so that 
\begin{align*}
  \delta = \sbrac{\delta_{\cS}, \delta_{\bar{\cS}}}^{\top}.
\end{align*}
Based on this new order, we can reorder $\mC \otimes \mI_m$ in the same way, obtaining 
\begin{align*}
  \mH = \begin{bmatrix}
    \mH_{\cS\cS} & \mH_{\cS\bar{\cS}} \\
    \mH_{\bar{\cS}\cS} & \mH_{\bar{\cS}\bar{\cS}} \\
  \end{bmatrix}
\end{align*}
where $\mH_{\cS\cS}$ means that rows in $\cS$ and columns in $\cS$, and others have similar meanings.
We will often refer to this as the Hessian surrogate or the curvature matrix.
Plug this into the original loss inequality (notice that change of order does not change the loss), we obtain 
\[
  \cL(\delta) = \delta^\top \mH \delta = \begin{bmatrix}
    \delta_\cS \\
    \delta_{\bar \cS}
  \end{bmatrix}^{\!\top} \begin{bmatrix}
    \mH_{\cS\cS} & \mH_{\cS\bar \cS} \\
    \mH_{\bar \cS \cS} & \mH_{\bar \cS \bar \cS}
  \end{bmatrix} \begin{bmatrix}
    \delta_\cS \\
    \delta_{\bar \cS}
  \end{bmatrix}.
\]
Expanding it, we get 
\[
  \cL(\delta) = \delta_\cS^\top \mH_{\cS\cS} \,\delta_\cS \;+\;
  2\,\delta_{\bar \cS}^\top \mH_{\bar \cS \cS}\,\delta_\cS \;+\;
  \delta_{\bar \cS}^\top \mH_{\bar \cS \bar \cS}\,\delta_{\bar \cS}.
\]
Now, notice that we can treat $\delta_{\cS}$ as constant (assume that we have already selected the coordinates in some way, which we will dicuss later, and because the values of those coordinates are fixed (as $-\omega_i$ to make the original parameter zero.), they are constants), we can take the gradient w.r.t. to $\delta_{\bar{\cS}}$. \footnote{We can think of this as a simplification on purpose: originally, we have to select which of the coordinates to prune and by how much on purpose, but now we choose to do it in a sequential manner, i.e., we first select the group of parameters to prune according to some standard, which allows us to solve the subsequent optimization problem via standard tools.}
As a result, we can write the minimization problem as 
\begin{align*}
  \min_{\delta_{\bar \cS}} \cL(\delta_{\bar{\cS}}; \delta_{\cS}). 
\end{align*}  
Notice that for this specific problem, we can actually use the optimality condition 
\begin{align*}
  2\mH_{\bar{\cS} \bar{\cS}} \delta_{\bar \cS} + 2\mH_{\bar{\cS}\cS} \delta_{\cS} = 0,
\end{align*}
Solving gives 
\begin{align*}
  \delta_{\bar{\cS}}^\star = -\mH_{\bar{\cS}\bar{\cS}}^{-1} \mH_{\bar{\cS}\cS}\delta_{\cS} = \mH_{\bar{\cS}\bar{\cS}}^{-1} \mH_{\bar{\cS}\cS}\omega_{\cS}.
\end{align*}
Note that the the matrix $\mH_{\bar{\cS}\bar{cS}}$, while PSD, is not necessarily invertible.
In practice what we do is that we add a $\lambda \cdot \mI$, which is actually equivalent to adding a regularizer $\lambda \cdot \norm{\delta_{\bar{\cS}}}^2$ to the objective.
This is often refers to as \textbf{damping}.
The final weights are now given by 
\begin{align*}
  \omega^\prime_{\cS} = 0, \qquad \omega^\prime_{\bar{\cS}} = \omega_{\bar{\cS}} + \delta^\star_{\bar{\cS}}.
\end{align*}

Now, we answer the remaining question of which of the parameters we wants to prune.
This is done with approximations using a cheap importance score.

\textbf{Importance of pruning a single weight:} If we prune only coordinate $i$, i.e., 
\begin{align*}
  \delta_i = -\omega_i, \quad \delta_j = 0 (j \neq i)
\end{align*}
then we have 
\begin{align*}
  \cL(\delta) = \omega_i^2\mH_{ii},
\end{align*}
We will treat this as the importance score, and the larger it is, the more sensitive this direction is (notice that it is always PSD), and we should be less willing to prune this.

In large LLMs, $\mH$ is huge typically, so we actually divide it into small blocks (e.g., $128 \times 128$) and compute the importance scores for weights in that block, and prune the lowest-scoring weights to hit the sparsity target.

A final remark on sparseGPT is that this type of pruning actually introduces unstructured sparsity, which is not useful for hardware acceleration.
However, it is not designed for this purpose in the begining.

\textbullet~ \textbf{GPTQ:} The idea is very similar to sparseGPT.
Imagine for a linear layer $\mW$, we want to quantized it to replace $\mW$ by a quantized matrix $\mQ_{\mW}$.
Define the perturbation, 
\begin{align*}
  \Delta \mW = \mQ_{\mW} - \mW, \qquad \delta = \vecc{\Delta \mW}.
\end{align*}
We want to minimize the output distortion 
\begin{align*}
  \cL(\delta) = \Exp{\norm{\Delta\mW x}^2} \simeq \delta^{\top}\mH\delta, 
\end{align*}
with certain constraints (instead of zero, we put on some grid) on $\mQ_{\mW}$, we can therefore use a similar way to approximate this optimization process.


\textbullet~ \textbf{SparseGPT-variants}: With nearly the same mathematics, SparseGPT can be extended to multiple other sparsity pattern.
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Block sparsity:} Fixed size blocks (e.g. $8 \times 8$, $16 \times 16$ are either kept or removed), sometimes the block sizes can be asymmetric, and typically, $50-75\%$ sparsity.
  Mainly in GPU-oriented research and less common then $N:M$ sparsity.

  \item \textbf{N:M sparsity:} one of the most important parttern today (as of 2025), which is semi-structured.
  An example would be 2:4 sparsity, which means that in every 4 weights, exactly 2 are zero.

  One may wonder why such a pattern leads to acceleration.
  In fact, the hardware (Ampere, Hopper) knows that in every contiguous group of 4 weights along a specific dimension, exactly 2 must be zero in advance.
  The key is that the groups are contiguous in memory and the hardware encodes is aware of a compacted mask, so it could reduce the number of FMA (Fused-Multiply-Add) operations.

  The Nvidia Tensor Cores have dedicated spares MMA instructions which half the multiplications.
  This results in $1.5 \sim 2$ speedups.
\end{enumerate}


\textbullet~ \textbf{Wanda:} Wanda is a first order, activation-aware pruning method that esitmates weights importance using only activation statistics and weight magnitudes, without any second-order (Hessian) compensation.

SparseGPT shows that second order pruning works extremely well, but it is computationally heavy because of damping and inverse.
A natural question based on that is that: 
\begin{verbatim}
  Can we get most of the benefit of SparseGPT without computing or
  inverting any Hessian?
\end{verbatim}
Let us consider the same linear layer with $\mW$, sparseGPT uses $\omega_i \mH_{ii}$ as the importance score, note that 
\begin{align*}
  \mH_{ii} = \Exp{x_j^2}, 
\end{align*}
for the corresponding input channel.
This is justifed, since that for each coordinate $i$ in $\omega$, there must be a corresponding $(r, j)$ in $\mW$ where $r$ is the index in the out dimension and $j$ is the index in the in dimension, we have $i = (j-1)m + r$.
Thus, 
\begin{align*}
  \mH_{ii} = \mC_{jj} = \Exp{x_j^2},
\end{align*}
where $x \in \R^d$ is the input.
\textbf{Wanda} makes the following approximation: $\mH_{ii} \simeq \Exp{\lvert x_j \rvert}$, i.e. 
\begin{align*}
  \mH_{ii} \simeq \norm{\mX_{j,:}}_1 \text{    or   } \mH_{ii} \simeq \norm{\mX_{j,:}}_2,
\end{align*} 
i.e, we take all $i$-th coordinates over the calibration batch and use $1$ or $2$ norm.
And we replace the original pruning score as. 
\begin{align*}
  \text{score}_i = \lvert \omega_i \rvert \norm{\mX_{j,:}}.
\end{align*}
After we determine which set of the parameters to prune, we do the pruning, and thats it, no compensation. 
Roughly speaking, Wanda is effectively replacing the sparseGPT score $\omega^2_i \mH_ii$ with $\lvert\omega_i\rvert \cdot \sqrt{\mH_ii}$, which is just a first order proxy of the second order sparseGPT criterion.
Now that we do not adjust the weights of the unpruned.
This works empirically, and the intuition is roughly that this is just first order approximations.


\subsection{Low-Precision Inference Techniques for LLMs}

Beyond model architecture and algorithmic improvements, precision reduction has emerged as a key strategy to reduce memory footprint, improve throughput, and lower inference cost.

\paragraph{AWQ:}
Activation-Aware Weight Quanization (AWQ), is a widely adopted post-training quantization method and is supported by several production inference frameworks, including vLLM and TensorRT-LLM.
It is commonly used for INT4-weight only inference on GPUs \footnote{This refers to that model weights are stored in $4$ bits integers and activations and most computations remain in higher precision (FP16 / BF16 / sometimes INT 8), this setup is executed efficiently on GPUs.}.

\textbf{Motivation:}

\subsection{Softmax Tricks}
The usual softmax of a vector $\mx$, is given by the following formula:
\begin{align*}
  \operatorname{softmax}(\mx)_i = \frac{e^{\mx_i}}{\sum_{j=1}^{d} e^{\mx_j}}.
\end{align*}
The problem is, if certain $x_i$ are too large, $e^{x_i}$ will overflow and become ``inf'', which causes trouble in training stability.

\paragraph{Safe softmax trick:} This refers to the following practice, 
\begin{align*}
  \operatorname{softmax}(\mx)_i = \frac{e^{\mx_i - m}}{\sum_{j=1}^{d} e^{\mx_j - m}}, \quad \text{where  } m \eqdef \max_i \mx_i.
\end{align*}
The two are equivalent in mathmatics, because we only divide each term by $e^m$, every $e^{\mx_i - m}$ is between $[0, 1]$, and the overflow problem is much more controllable.

\paragraph{The log-sum-exp trick:}
Now, if we imagine that we have obtained a logit vector $\mx$, we first compute the softmax to transform it into a probability vector, then take the log and finally we compute the negative log likelihood as the cross entropy, basically, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \operatorname{NLL}(\mx) = - \log \frac{e^{\mx_y}}{\sum_j e^{\mx_j}},
\end{align*}
where we actually faces the same problem as before.
But notice we can simplify the expression a bit, by 
\begin{align*}
  - \log \frac{e^{\mx_y}}{\sum_j e^{\mx_j}} &= - \rbrac{\mx_y + \log\frac{1}{\sum_j e^{\mx_j}}} \\
  &= -\rbrac{\mx_y + \log\frac{1}{e^{m}\sum_j e^{\mx_j - m}}} \\
  &= -\rbrac{\mx_y - m + \log \frac{1}{\sum_j e^{\mx_j - m}}} \\
  &= -\mx_y + m + \log \sum_j e^{\mx_j - m}.
\end{align*}


\subsection{KL divergence and Cross Entropy}
Let us first give the definition, let $\mq, \mpp$ be a discrete probability distribution over the vocabulary $\cV$, then we have the term entropy defined as  
\begin{align}
  H(\mq) = - \sum_{v \in \cV} \mq(v) \cdot \log \mq(v). \tag{Entropy}
\end{align} 
and cross entropy defined as 
\begin{align}
  H(\mq, \mpp) = - \sum_{v \in \cV} \mq(v) \log \mpp(v). \tag{Cross Entropy}
\end{align}
The KL divergence is defined as 
\begin{align}
  \text{KL}(\mq || \mpp) = \sum_{v \in \cV} \mq(v) \log \frac{\mq(v)}{\mpp(v)}. \tag{KL Divergence}
\end{align}
It is easy to check that the three definitions satisfies 
\begin{align}
  \label{eq:relation-kl-cross}
  \text{KL}(\mq || \mpp) = -H(\mq) + H(\mq, \mpp).
\end{align}

One may notice that in the cross entropy loss, we use $\mq$ as the goal (data-generating) distribution, and $\mpp$ as the model / parametric distribution, hence, minimizing the cross entropy loss is just equivalent to minimizing the KL divergence via \eqref{eq:relation-kl-cross}.
To this end, one can interpret the minimization as a type of projection based on KL divergence.


\subsection{Contiguous}
Many PyTorch operations (especially `.view') assume the tensor is laid out contiguously in memory.
If it isn't, the operation either fails or silently does extra work (copies), which affects correctness assumptions and performance.

\paragraph{Definition:}
A tensor is contiguous if its elements are stored in memory in the same order as its logical indexing.
Example:
\begin{lstlisting}
  x = tensor( [[a, b, c],
              [d, e, f]] )
\end{lstlisting}
It has a memory lay out of 
\begin{lstlisting}
  [a, b, c, d, e, f],
\end{lstlisting}
which is contiguous.

\paragraph{How tensors become non-contiguous:}
Slicing, transposing, permuting does not move data, it only changes strides.
\begin{lstlisting}
  x = torch.randn(4, 5, 6)
  y = x[:, :-1, :]      # slicing
  z = x.transpose(1, 2)
\end{lstlisting}
Both y and z are usually non-contiguous. 
They are views pointing to the same memory, but with different strides.

\paragraph{.view()}
.view() is a pure reinterpretation of memory.
It requires: new shape is compatible with existing strides.
\begin{lstlisting}
  x = torch.randn(2, 3, 4)
  y = x[:, :-1, :]      # non-contiguous
  y.view(-1, 4)         # RuntimeError
\end{lstlisting}
Because the memory layout is no longer ``linear'' in the way .view() expects.
\begin{lstlisting}
  y = y.contiguous()
  y.view(-1, 4)
\end{lstlisting}
works because .contiguous() physically copies the data into a contiguous block.

\paragraph{.reshape()}
It is kind of smarter in the sense that in the case that the tensor is contiguous, it returns a view (zero cost), but in the other cases, it will silently allocate a contiguous copy.
However, if we use this function, we lose control of when memory is allocated, how much memory is allocated and performance predictability.
That's why we often write 
\begin{lstlisting}
  y = y.contiguous().view(...)
\end{lstlisting}
to make the cost explicit.

All in one sentence: If we slice / transpose / permute a tensor and then call .view(), we should almost always call .contiguous() first.

\paragraph{Stride:}
Strides are the rule that tells PyTorch how to walk through memory when we index a tensor.
For a tensor $\mx$ with shape $(d_0, d1, \hdots, d_n)$, stride at dimension $k$ is how many memory elements we must skip to move the index by $+1$ along that dimension.
If $\mx$ has base pointer $\text{ptr}$ and strides $s = (s_0, s_1, \hdots, s_n)$ then the address of $\mx\sbrac{i_0, i_1, \hdots, i_n}$ is given by 
\begin{align*}
  \text{ptr} + \sum_{k=0}^{n}i_k \cdot s_k.
\end{align*}


\subsection{Binary Classification, AUC and ROC}
In binary classification tasks, we often construct the confusion matrix, which has the following four categories:
\begin{itemize}
  \item TP: Ground Truth Positive and Prediction Positive
  \item FP: Ground Truth Negative and Prediction Positive
  \item TN: Ground Truth Positive and Prediction Negative
  \item FN: Ground Truth Negative and Prediction Negative
\end{itemize}
We define two key rates 
\begin{itemize}
  \item Recall: (True Positive Rate) 
  \begin{align*}
    \text{TPR} = \frac{\text{TP}}{\text{TP} + \text{FN}}
  \end{align*}
  This stands for the percentage of TP in the total correct examples.

  \item Sensitivity: (False Positive Rate)
  \begin{align*}
    \text{FPR} = \frac{\text{FP}}{\text{FP} + \text{TN}}
  \end{align*}
  This stands for the percentage of FP in the total wrong examples.
\end{itemize}

Then the ROC (Receiver Operating Characteristic) curve is the curve that 
\begin{itemize}
  \item $x$ axis: False Positive Rate (FPR)
  \item $y$ axis: True Positive Rate (TPR)
\end{itemize}
As the threshold go from $0$ to $1$ (assume the score output by the model is in $[0, 1]$), FPR, TPR changes thats why we get a curve, for threshold $0$, we predict all positive, so $y$ should be $1$ (because $\text{FN} = 0$), and $x$ should be $1$ (because $\text{TN} = 0$). 
When we set threshold to be $1$ (all negative predictions), we get $(0, 0)$

AUC (Area under the ROC curve) measures how well a model ranks positive examples above negative ones, i.e., AUC is the probability that a randomly chosen positive sample gets a higher score than a randomly chosen negative sample.
Mathematically:
\begin{align*}
  \text{AUC} = \int_{0}^{1} \text{TPR}(\text{FPR})\, \textbf{d}\, \text{FPR}.
\end{align*}
Now notice that each in FPR corresponds to letting one more negative pass the threshold (FP increases, and FPR increases), the corresponding TPR tells you how many positives are already above that negative.
\begin{align*}
  \text{TPR}(t) &= \frac{\lvert\cbrac{x^+: s(x^{+}) \geq t}\rvert}{\text{P}} \\
  \text{FPR}(t) &= \frac{\lvert\cbrac{x^+: s(x^{+}) < t} \rvert}{N}
\end{align*}
Fix a ground-truth negative sample $x^-$ with score $s^-$, at the threshold $t = s^-$, this negative just becomes predicted positive so $\text{FPR}$ increases $1/\text{N}$, and if we plug in $t = s^-$, we realize $\text{TPR}$ becomes the fraction of postive samples that has a higher score than this negative example.
Since each negative contributes:
\begin{align*}
  \Delta \text{FPR} = \frac{1}{\text{N}},
\end{align*}
Assume no ties, the integral becomes a summation of 
\begin{align*}
  \text{AUC} = \frac{1}{\text{N}} \sum_{x^-} \frac{\lvert \cbrac{x^+ : s(x^+) > s(x^-) }\rvert}{\text{P}} = \frac{1}{\text{PN}}\sum_{x^-}\lvert \cbrac{x^+: s(x^+) > s(x^-) }\rvert
\end{align*}
The numerator counts all positive negative pairs where $s(x^+) > s(x^-)$ and the denominator is the total number of pairs.
Thus 
\begin{align*}
  \text{AUC} = \text{Pr}\cbrac{s(x^+) > s(x^-)},
\end{align*}
because we first computed for each $x^-$ the probability of a $x^{+}$ that has a larger $s(x^{+}) \geq s(x^{-})$, then average over $x^-$.

\paragraph{The case where ties happens:}
It could happen that $s(x^+) = s(x^-)$, in this case, we define 
\begin{align*}
  \phi(x^+, x^-) = \begin{cases}
    1, &\quad s(x^+) > s(x^-) \\
    \frac{1}{2}, &\quad s(x^+) = s(x^-) \\
    0, &\quad s(x^+) < s(x^-).
  \end{cases}
\end{align*}
Then AUC becomes 
\begin{align*}
  \text{AUC} = \frac{1}{\text{PN}} \sum_{x^+}\sum_{x^-} \phi(x^+, x^-) = \text{Pr}\cbrac{s(x^+) > s(x^-)} + \frac{1}{2}\text{Pr}\cbrac{s(x^+) = s(x^-)}.
\end{align*}

\paragraph{Practical Hint.}
Assume we have the ground truth labels $y_i \in \cbrac{0, 1}$ and the model score $s_i \in \R$.
\begin{itemize}
  \item Sort all samples decreasingly in terms of their $s_i$.
  \item Move threshold from $+\infty$ to each point $s_i$ and compute $(\text{FPR}_k, \text{TPR}_k)$.
  \item Compute the area, given ROC points we have generated, we have 
  \begin{align*}
    \text{AUC} = \sum_k (\text{FPR}_{k+1} - \text{FPR}_k) * \frac{\text{TPR}_k + \text{TPR}_{k+1}}{2}.
  \end{align*}
\end{itemize}

We may use the Mann-Whiteney formula (uses the pairwise ranking view)
\begin{itemize}
  \item Rank all samples by score and record their ranking $r_i$, with average ranks are used for ties.
  \item \begin{align*}
    \text{AUC} = \frac{\text{R}_+ - \frac{\text{P}\rbrac{\text{P} + 1}}{2}}{\text{PN}}
  \end{align*}
  where $\text{R}_+$ is sum of ranks of positive samples, which is mathematically identical to ROC integration and very efficient.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}
  from sklearn.metrics import roc_auc_score
  auc = roc_auc_score(labels.cpu(), scores.cpu())
\end{lstlisting}
Remark: Random guessing gives AUC around $0.5$ numerically.


\subsection{Random Interview Questions}

\paragraph{Write the GRPO loss.}

Instead of learning a value function, GPRO uses relative ranking
inside the group, that is, for an prompt $\mx$, we prepare a group of rollouts $\my_i$, and we denote 
\begin{align*}
  A_i = r(\my_i, \mx) - \frac{1}{G}\sum_{i=1}^{G}r(\my_i, \mx),
\end{align*}
where $G$ is the size of the group.
We optionally normalized them with 
\begin{align*}
  A_i = \frac{A_i}{\sqrt{\operatorname{Var}(A) + \varepsilon}}.
\end{align*}
The GRPO loss can be written as 
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{GRPO}} = -\frac{1}{G}\sum_{i=1}^{G}\frac{\pi_{\theta}(\my_i \mid \mx)}{\pi_{\thetaold}(\my_i \mid \mx)} A_i
\end{align*}
In practice sometimes we clipped the noise 
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{GRPO-CLIP}} = -\frac{1}{G}\sum_{i=1}^{G} \min\cbrac{\rho_iA_i, \operatorname{Clip}(\rho_i, 1 - \varepsilon, 1 + \varepsilon)A_i},
\end{align*}
where $\rho_i = \frac{\pi_{\theta}(\my_i \mid \mx)}{\pi_{\thetaold}(\my_i \mid \mx)}$.
Very commonly, we apply KL divergence to the loss 
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{total}} = \cL_{\text{GRPO}} + \beta \cdot \klD{\pi_\theta(\cdot \mid \mx)}{\pi_{\thetaref}(\cdot \mid \mx)}.
\end{align*}

\begin{itemize}
  \item Sample multiple candidate answers to the same interview question.
  \item Rank them relative to each other.
  \item Push probability up for answers better than the group average.
  \item Push probability down for worse ones.
  \item No value network, no reward baseline model.
\end{itemize}
GRPO (like PPO) performs multiple gradient update steps on rollouts generated by $\thetaold$, but only within a trust region.


\paragraph{Write the PPO loss.}
Given prompt $\mx_t$ sample response $\my_t$ and advantage $A_t$, let $\rho_t$ be 
\begin{align*}
  \rho_t = \frac{\pi_{\theta}(\my_t \mid \mx_t)}{\pi_{\thetaold}(\my_t \mid \mx_t)}.
\end{align*}
We can write $\cL_{\text{PPO}}$ as 
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{PPO}} = - \ExpSub{t}{\min \cbrac{\rho_t A_t, \operatorname{Clip}\rbrac{\rho_t, 1 - \varepsilon, 1 + \varepsilon}A_t}}
\end{align*}
We also add the KL to reference
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{total}} = \cL_{\text{PPO}} + \beta \cdot \ExpSub{t}{\klD{\pi_\theta(\cdot \mid \mx)}{\pi_{\thetaref}(\cdot \mid \mx)}}.
\end{align*}
$A_t$ is the $r(\mx_t, \my_t) - V_{\phi}(\mx_t)$ (how good above average), we use the value head to estimates the expected absolute reward for a prompt.
And we add the value (critic) loss
\begin{align*}
  \cL_{\text{value}} = \ExpSub{t}{\rbrac{V_{\phi}(\mx_t) - G_t}^2},
\end{align*}
where $G_t$ is the observed return from rollout with 
\begin{align*}
  \Exp{G_t \mid \mx_t} = V^{\pi}(x_t) = \ExpSub{\my \sim \pi}{r(x, y)}.
\end{align*}
We use this loss to update the value head instead of the policy.
In classical RL PPO, we add entropy bonus, but in LLM RL, this is replaced by KL divergence control.
In GRPO, we are basically replacing the estimated $V_{\phi}$ with the value head with the group average reard.
PPO learns what ``average'' means, GRPO computes it directly from the samples.


\paragraph{GRPO loss oscillates back and forth}

If GRPO loss oscillates back and forth, it usually means the updates are too noisy or too aggressive, or your advantage signal is unstable.

\textbullet~ \textbf{Updates are too aggressive (most common)}

\begin{itemize}
  \item Symptoms: 
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Loss spikes every few steps/epochs.
    \item KL to the reference (or to $\theta_old$) jumps.
    \item Policy starts changing too fast; group advantages flip sign frequently.
  \end{enumerate}

  \item Fixes:
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Lower learning rate (first thing to try)
    \item Fewer PPO-style update epochs per rollout (e.g., from 4-8 down to 1-2) (Note that this is similar to local training.)
    \item Tighter clipping: reduce $\varepsilon$ (e.g., 0.2 → 0.1 → 0.05).
    \item Add / increase KL penalty to a reference model; use adaptive KL if you have it.
    \item Early stop on KL: stop updates when KL exceeds a target.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

\textbullet~ \textbf{Group-relative advantage is too noisy}:
GRPO baseline is the group mean. If the group is small or low-diversity, the mean is a noisy estimator, so advantages bounce.

\begin{itemize}
  \item Reasons 
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Group size $G$ too small (e.g., 2-4).
    \item Sampling is too random (high temperature/top-p), making rewards noisy.
    \item Reward model is noisy or inconsistent for your task.
  \end{enumerate}

  \item Fixes 
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Increase group size $G$.
    \item Normalize advantages within each group.
    \item Clip advantages to avoid extreme updates.
    \item Stabilize sampling, slightly lower temperature, reduce top-p, or use a fixed decoding policy during training rollouts
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

\textbullet~ \textbf{Reward scale / reward hacking / reward distribution shift}

\begin{itemize}
  \item Symptoms:
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Rewards have heavy tails (rare huge scores)
    \item Rewards drift upward/downward rapidly across steps
    \item Loss oscillation correlates with reward outliers
  \end{enumerate}

  \item Solutions:
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Reward normalization (per batch or per prompt-group)
    \item Track and limit response length; uncontrolled length often destabilizes reward signals
    \item Winsorize rewards (cap extreme values)
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}


\subsection{About Fast Inferencing}
There are several techniques exist for fast inferencing in practice.

\textbullet~ (1) We have already talked about one of them, which is the V cache optimization.
When we perform autoregressive inferencing, the main cost of computing attention comes from reading the KV cache, with MQA / GQA, we can reduce the size of the KV cache by asking some of the heads to share Key vector and Value vector.

\textbullet~ (2) Another technique is the Paged KV Cache, which is used in vLLM.
This is a memory-management technique introduced in the vLLM inference system.
The key idea is to manage the KV cache like an operating system manages virtual memory pages rather than storing it as one long contiguous block.

Traditionally, most implementations allocate contiguous memory for each request.
Example memory layout:
\begin{verbatim}
Request A
[token1 token2 token3 token4 ... token120]

Request B
[token1 token2 ... token15]

Request C
[token1 ... token450]  
\end{verbatim}
Each sequence occupies one continuous block. 
However, in real inference servers: <1> requests arrive and finish at different times <2> sequences have different lengths.

Initial memory:
\begin{verbatim}
A: 120 tokens
B: 15 tokens
C: 450 tokens
D: 8 tokens  
\end{verbatim}

Memory after B finishes:
\begin{verbatim}
A: 120 tokens
[free space of 15 tokens]
C: 450 tokens
D: 8 tokens
\end{verbatim}
The system cannot use the 15-token hole, so it must allocate memory elsewhere. 
This is memory fragmentation, which results in GPU memory wasted, less requests catered and throughput decreases.
Instead of storing tokens contiguously, vLLM splits the KV cache into fixed-size pages.
Each request stores a list of pages, not one continuous block.
The physical pages can be anywhere.
This is exactly the same idea as virtual memory paging in operating systems.

This also enables continous batching (new requests can be inserted between decoding steps).

In naive batching: we do 
\begin{enumerate}
  \item collect a batch of requests
  \item run decoding for that batch
  \item wait until some finish
  \item build a new batch later
\end{enumerate}
So the batch is relatively static.

Example
\begin{verbatim}
Batch at start: A, B, C
decode step 1
decode step 2
decode step 3
...
\end{verbatim}
If a new request D arrives while A, B, C are already being decoded, the system may not insert D immediately. It may wait for some convenient rebatching point.

In continous batching, the server does this instead:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item after each decode step, it can
  \item remove finished requests
  \item add newly arrived requests
  \item keep unfinished requests
\end{enumerate}
This means the GPU is kept busy with a continuously refreshed set of sequences.
With the previous contiguous design, we need to make sure there are enough space because we do not have the concept of pages. 
This causes problem for continuous batching. 

\paragraph{About vLLM:}
vLLM is an inference and serving engine for LLMs. 
In plain English: it is a system we use after training, when we want to run a model efficiently for generation or expose it as an API. 
Its official docs position it around two main workflows: \textbf{offline batched inference} and \textbf{online serving} through an OpenAI-compatible server \footnote{lets your app call a local/self-hosted model almost like it calls the OpenAI API.}. 
It is known for high throughput and memory-efficient serving, with techniques like paged attention / paged KV cache.

Example usage:
\begin{lstlisting}
  from vllm import LLM, SamplingParams

  llm = LLM(model="facebook/opt-125m")
  sampling_params = SamplingParams(temperature=0.8, top_p=0.95, max_tokens=64)

  prompts = [
    "Explain what a transformer is in one paragraph.",
    "What is the difference between SGD and Adam?",
  ]

  outputs = llm.generate(prompts, sampling_params)

  for output in outputs:
    print(output.prompt)
    print(output.outputs[0].text)
\end{lstlisting}

\textbullet~ (3) Continous Batching: itself is a fast inferencing technique.

\textbullet~ (4) Flash Attention: It does not change the mathematical definition of attention, it only changes how attention is scheduled on GPU memory.

Let us consider a classical setting when we compute attention for one head: we have 
\begin{align*}
  \mQ, \mK, \mV \in \R^{L \times d}
\end{align*}
and the attention (scaled dot product attention) is computed as 
\begin{align*}
  \mS = \frac{\mQ\mK^{\top}}{\sqrt{d}}, \quad \mP = \text{softmax}(\mS), \quad \mO = \mP\mV.
\end{align*}
The problem is that if we look at $\mS$ and $\mP$, their sizes are $L \times L$, which means that they are huge, especially when the context lengths are long.

GPU has HBM\footnote{High Bandwidth Memory: very wide memory bus, many parallel channels} / global memory, which is large but slow, and it also has SRAM\footnote{Static Random Access Memory: This is on-chip memory, built directly on the GPU die.} / shared memory / registers which are much smaller but much faster.

In the naive implementation, we typically do things like:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item read $\mQ$ and $\mK$
  \item compute all scores $\mS$
  \item write $\mS$ out to HBM
  \item read $\mS$ back 
  \item compute softmax
  \item maybe write/read again
  \item multiply by $\mV$
\end{enumerate}
So even if the arithmetic is fine, the algorithm moves a lot of data back and forth between slow and fast memory. FlashAttention was explicitly introduced as an IO-aware exact attention algorithm to reduce these HBM reads/writes.

FlashAttention avoids materializing the attention matrix by computing the needed pieces block by block and immediately folds them into the final output.
The core idea is tiling.

\paragraph{Tiling.}
Instead of treating $\mQ, \mK, \mV$ as one large tensor, we partition them
along the sequence dimension
\[
\mQ=\begin{bmatrix}\mQ^{(1)}\\\mQ^{(2)}\\\vdots\end{bmatrix},\quad
\mK=\begin{bmatrix}\mK^{(1)}\\\mK^{(2)}\\\vdots\end{bmatrix},\quad
\mV=\begin{bmatrix}\mV^{(1)}\\\mV^{(2)}\\\vdots\end{bmatrix}.
\]
Each tile contains a contiguous chunk of tokens.

For a fixed query block $\mQ^{(i)}$, we iterate over the key/value tiles
$\mK^{(j)}$ and $\mV^{(j)}$ and compute the score tile
\begin{align*}
\mS^{(i,j)} = \frac{\mQ^{(i)}(\mK^{(j)})^\top}{\sqrt{d}} .
\end{align*}

Instead of forming the full attention matrix
\[
\mS=\frac{\mQ\mK^\top}{\sqrt{d}},
\]
the computation proceeds tile by tile so that the working data fits in
on-chip memory.

\paragraph{Softmax.}
Attention probabilities are obtained by applying a row-wise softmax
\begin{align*}
\mP = \operatorname{softmax}(\mS).
\end{align*}

For a single query row $r$ the attention weights are
\begin{align*}
p_{rj}
=
\frac{e^{S_{rj}}}{\sum_{t=1}^{T} e^{S_{rt}}},
\qquad j=1,\dots,T.
\end{align*}

The denominator involves all keys, so it cannot be computed independently
within each tile.

\paragraph{Online Softmax.}
To avoid materializing the entire score row, we process the keys
sequentially and maintain two running statistics
\begin{align*}
m_t &= \max_{1\le j\le t} S_{rj}, \\
l_t &= \sum_{j=1}^{t} e^{S_{rj}-m_t},
\qquad t=1,\dots,T .
\end{align*}

Assume that after processing keys $1,\dots,t-1$ we have
\begin{align*}
m_{t-1} &= \max_{1\le j<t} S_{rj}, \\
l_{t-1} &= \sum_{j=1}^{t-1} e^{S_{rj}-m_{t-1}} .
\end{align*}

When a new score $S_{rt}$ is observed, the running maximum becomes
\begin{align*}
m_t = \max(m_{t-1}, S_{rt}).
\end{align*}

The normalization term satisfies
\begin{align*}
l_t
&=
\sum_{j=1}^{t} e^{S_{rj}-m_t} \\
&=
e^{m_{t-1}-m_t} l_{t-1}
+
e^{S_{rt}-m_t}.
\end{align*}

\paragraph{Attention Output.}
The attention output for query $r$ is
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{o}_r
=
\frac{\sum_{j=1}^{T} e^{S_{rj}} \mathbf{v}_j}
{\sum_{j=1}^{T} e^{S_{rj}}}.
\end{align*}
Note that this is basically $\text{softmax}(\mS)\mV$.
Define the running numerator (numerically stable)
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{o}_t
=
\sum_{j=1}^{t} e^{S_{rj}-m_t} \mathbf{v}_j .
\end{align*}

Using the same rescaling argument,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{o}_t
=
e^{m_{t-1}-m_t}\mathbf{o}_{t-1}
+
e^{S_{rt}-m_t}\mathbf{v}_t .
\end{align*}

After all keys have been processed, the final attention output is
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{o}_r = \frac{\mathbf{o}_T}{l_T}.
\end{align*}

In practice the algorithm applies this update simultaneously to every
row of the current query tile while iterating over key/value tiles.
The full attention matrix is never materialized; only the current tile
and the running statistics are maintained.

It is worth noting that the memory consumption changes from $\cO(T^2)$ to $\cO(T)$, this is because in FlashAttention, we store:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item $\mQ, \mK, \mV$: already part of the model computation
  \item output $\mO \in \R^{T \times d}$
  \item per-row running statistics like max and normalization
  \item one or a few temporary tiles in SRAM
\end{enumerate} 
So the extra memory for the attention computation scales linearly in $T$ not quadratically.
The papers are precise that the savings come from reducing HBM traffic and not materializing the full attention matrix.
However, the computation is still roughly \textbf{quadratic}.
What changes is memory footprint and IO cost, which is often the real bottleneck on GPU. So FlashAttention is better thought of as: same exact attention math, much better memory movement pattern.

\paragraph{Why recomputation appears?}
Because FlashAttention refuses to store large intermediates, in backward pass it may recompute some local quantities instead of saving everything from forward. This is a classic time-vs-memory tradeoff, but on GPUs it often wins because memory traffic is so expensive. The original paper emphasizes that reducing memory reads/writes can outperform approaches that try to save every intermediate.


\paragraph{Three generations of FlashAttention}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{FlashAttention:} The original 2022 work introduced the tiled, IO-aware exact attention algorithm that avoids materializing the full attention matrix and substantially reduces HBM traffic.
  
  \item \textbf{FlashAttention-2:} keeps the same basic philosophy but reorganize the kernel so the GPU stays busy much more of the time. 
  What FlashAttention-2 changes is how the work is partitioned and scheduled on the GPU, so it runs much faster in practice.
  \begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Fewer non-matmul operations:} more time is spent in tensor-core-friendly matrix multiplications and less time is wasted on bookkeeping, rescaling, and other operations that GPUs execute less efficiently.
    
    \item \textbf{Better parallelism across the sequence dimension:} FlashAttention-1 can underutilize the GPU when batch size or number of heads is small. FlashAttention-2 parallelizes attention computation more aggressively, including for a single head, across multiple thread blocks, so GPU occupancy is higher.
    
    \item \textbf{Better work partitioning within a thread block:} Inside each block, FlashAttention-2 distributes work across warps\footnote{A warp is a group of 32 threads that execute the same instruction at the same time on an NVIDIA GPU. When you launch a CUDA kernel with many threads, the GPU hardware internally schedules them in blocks of 32-thread bundles, called warps. The GPU scheduler operates at the warp level, not the thread level.} more carefully, which reduces synchronization and shared-memory traffic. That means less internal overhead and better effective speed.
  \end{itemize}
  The FlashAttention-2 paper reports roughly 2x speedup over FlashAttention-1 in many settings, and says it reaches about 50-73\% of theoretical peak FLOPs/s on A100, which is much closer to GEMM-like efficiency.
  
  \item \textbf{FlashAttention-3:} FlashAttention-3 targets Hopper GPUs such as H100 and uses Hopper-specific features. 
  (1) exploiting asynchrony and TMA\footnote{Tensor Memory Accelerator} to overlap compute and data movement, 
  (2) interleaving blockwise matmul and softmax operations more aggressively, and 
  (3) using low-precision support such as FP8-related techniques.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Warp:}
A warp is a group of 32 threads that execute the same instruction at the same time on an NVIDIA GPU. When you launch a CUDA kernel with many threads, the GPU hardware internally schedules them in blocks of 32-thread bundles, called warps. The GPU scheduler operates at the warp level, not the thread level.

Suppose a warp runs this kernel:
\begin{verbatim}
  y[i] = a * x[i] + b
\end{verbatim}
Warp execution looks conceptually like
\begin{verbatim}
Thread 0   : y[0]  = a*x[0]  + b
Thread 1   : y[1]  = a*x[1]  + b
...
Thread 31  : y[31] = a*x[31] + b
\end{verbatim}
But the GPU issues one instruction for the whole warp.
Because warps share the same instruction stream, branches can hurt performance.
Example:
\begin{verbatim}
if (x[i] > 0)
  y[i] = x[i]
else
  y[i] = 0
\end{verbatim}
If inside a warp, some threads take the if, others take the else, then GPU executes both paths sequentially, masking threads.
This is called warp divergence.
When people say: FlashAttention-2 distributes work across warps more carefully, they mean the kernel assigns computation like query blocks, key blocks, reduction work to different warps, instead of having one warp doing sequential steps.

The original FlashAttention had patterns like:
\begin{verbatim}
warp 0:
  load K tile
  compute partial attention
  update running softmax
  write results
\end{verbatim}
FlashAttention-2 reorganizes so that
\begin{verbatim}
warp 0 → compute QK^T
warp 1 → compute softmax stats
warp 2 → multiply V
warp 3 → reductions
\end{verbatim}
This improves warp occupancy, memory throughput and SM utilization.

The execution hierarchy is
\begin{verbatim}
GPU
 - SM (streaming multiprocessor)
     - thread blocks
         - warps (32 threads)
             - threads
\end{verbatim}
FlashAttention kernels are tuned around: warp-level matrix multiply, warp-level reductions and warp-level memory loads.
FlashAttention-2 improved performance largely by designing the kernel around warp-level parallelism instead of thread-level loops.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{ll}
\hline
\textbf{GPU Level} & \textbf{CPU Analogy} \\
\hline
thread & scalar instruction \\
warp & vector instruction \\
block & CPU core workload \\
SM & CPU core \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{A useful mental model relating CUDA execution hierarchy to CPU concepts.}
\end{table}


\textbullet~ (5) Speculative Decoding:
The core idea is 
\begin{enumerate}
  \item a small fast model (the draft model) proposes several next tokens at once
  \item the large target model then checks those proposals in parallel
  \item every proposal that passes verification is accepted
  \item generation then continues from the first rejection point or from the end of the accepted chunk.
\end{enumerate}
So instead of the big model doing one token per forward pass, speculative decoding tries to let the small model ``guess ahead'' a few steps, and then uses one target-model pass to verify multiple guesses at once.
That is why the technique can \textbf{xreduce inter-token latency}, especially in memory-bound inference settings.

The speed up depends on (1) draft cost: The draft model must be much cheaper than the target model;
(2) Acceptance rate: The draft model's guesses must often be accepted.


\textbullet~ (6) Quantization: \textbf{Reduce memory bandwidth}.

\textbullet~ (7) Kernel Fusion: Kernel fusion means combining several GPU operations that would normally run as separate kernels into one kernel. TensorRT's glossary defines fusion exactly in this spirit: combining multiple layers into a single operation to reduce\textbf{memory bandwidth} use and \textbf{kernel launch overhead}.

\textbullet~ (8) Prefill / Decode Separation: 
LLM inference is not one uniform workload. It has two very different phases:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Prefill}: consume the whole prompt and build the KV cache.
  \item \textbf{Decode}: generate new tokens one by one using that KV cache.
\end{enumerate}
These two phases stress the hardware in very different ways, so good systems often treat them differently in scheduling, batching, kernels, and even cluster layout.
vLLM explicitly supports this idea; its docs describe disaggregated prefilling as running prefill and decode in different vLLM instances, so we can optimize TTFT(Time To First Token)\footnote{Users care about at least two latency metrics: TTFT: time to first token, ITL: inter-token latency} and inter-token latency separately. vLLM also documents chunked prefill and different thresholds for prefill-heavy versus decode-only batches.

\textbullet~ (9) Tensor Parallel Inference: Megatron / TensorRT-LLM. Multiple GPUs assigned for tensor parallelism.

\textbullet~ (10) Activation Recomputation Removal.

\textbullet~ (11) Prefix Cache: If multiple requests share the same prefix (e.g. system prompt), we can cache KV cache. Often seen in ChatGPT, vLLM.


\textbf{LLM inference is often memory-bound: Read model weights; Read KV cache. Training is often compute bound because of large batches.}


\subsection{About PPO}
In RLHF, we usually have three stages:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Supervised fine-tuning (SFT):} Train the language model on high-quality demonstrations.
  \item \textbf{Reward model (RM):} Train a model to score outputs according to human preferences.
  \item \textbf{RL optimization with PPO:} Further update the policy model so it generates responses with higher reward, while not drifting too far from the original SFT model.
\end{enumerate}

\textbullet~ The policy is the language model itself: $\pi_\theta(\my \mid \mx)$, where $\mx$ is the prompt and $\my$ is the generated response.

\textbullet~ \textbf{Vanilla policy gradient} can be unstable: one update can change the model too much and destroy language quality. 
\textbf{PPO} is a stabilized policy-gradient method that prevents overly large policy updates.

\textbullet~ RLHF objective: Denote the reward model as $r_\phi(\mx, \my)$ and reference model $\pi_{\text{ref}}$, a common scalar reward is 
\begin{align*}
  R(\mx,\my)=r_{\phi}(\mx,\my)-\beta\,\mathrm{KL}\!\left(\pi_{\theta}(\cdot\mid\mx)\,\|\,\pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(\cdot\mid\mx)\right).
\end{align*}
or in practice often a token-level KL penalty approximation such as
\begin{align*}
  R(\mx,\my)\approx r_{\phi}(\mx,\my)-\beta\sum_{t=1}^{T}\!\left[\log \pi_{\theta}(y_t\mid \mx,\my_{<t})-\log \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(y_t\mid \mx,\my_{<t})\right].
\end{align*}
The KL penalty stops the model from deviate too far from the reference.
We will not use the reward directly, having a reward (or return) is not enough for stable policy gradient updates, and the advantage $A$ reduces variance and tells the optimizer whether an action was better or worse than expected.
So we use reward to estimate $A$.

\textbullet~ PPO surrogate objective: Suppose data is collected using the old policy $\pi_{\text{old}}$: Define the importance ratio
\begin{align*}
  \rho_t(\theta)=\frac{\pi_{\theta}(y_t\mid \mx,\my_{<t})}{\pi_{\theta_{\text{old}}}(y_t\mid \mx,\my_{<t})}.
\end{align*}
Let $\hat{A}_t$ be the estimated advantage at token $t$. PPO maximizes
\[
  L_{\mathrm{PPO}}(\theta)=\mathbb{E}\left[\min\left(\rho_t(\theta)\hat{A}_t,\;\mathrm{clip}\left(\rho_t(\theta),1-\epsilon,1+\epsilon\right)\hat{A}_t\right)\right].
\]
This clipping is the defining trick.
If $\rho_t(\theta)$ becomes too large or too small, PPO truncates the incentive to push it further.
So even if the gradient wants to make a huge update, PPO resists that.

\textbullet~ Where does the advantage come from? In RLHF for LLMs, after sampling a completion, one must convert the sequence-level reward into learning signals for the policy. There are three common ways to construct the advantage.

\paragraph{Sequence-level handling.}
A simple approximation treats the entire completion as a single action.  
After sampling
\[
  y=(y_1,\dots,y_T), \qquad y \sim \pi_\theta(\cdot \mid x),
\]
the reward model produces a scalar score
\[
  r_\phi(x,y).
\]
A KL penalty against a frozen reference policy $\pi_{\mathrm{ref}}$ is added
\[
  \mathrm{KL}(x,y)
  =
  \sum_{t=1}^{T}
  \bigl(
  \log \pi_\theta(y_t \mid x,y_{<t})
  -
  \log \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(y_t \mid x,y_{<t})
  \bigr).
\]
The sequence-level reward becomes
\[
  R(x,y)=r_\phi(x,y)-\beta\,\mathrm{KL}(x,y).
\]
A baseline or value estimate $V_\psi(x)$ predicts the expected reward for prompt $x$, giving the sequence-level advantage
\[
  \hat A(x,y)=R(x,y)-V_\psi(x).
\]
In practice this scalar advantage is often broadcast to all tokens in the sampled completion when updating the policy.

\paragraph{Token-level returns without GAE.}
More commonly, the sequence reward is converted into token-level rewards.
Define the token-level state
\[
  s_t=(x,y_{<t}),
  \]
  and compute the per-token KL
  \[
  \mathrm{KL}_t
  =
  \log \pi_\theta(y_t \mid s_t)
  -
  \log \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(y_t \mid s_t).
  \]
  The token rewards are constructed as
  \[
  r_t =
  \begin{cases}
  -\beta\,\mathrm{KL}_t, & t<T,\\
  r_\phi(x,y)-\beta\,\mathrm{KL}_T, & t=T.
  \end{cases}
\]
From these we compute the return
\[
  G_t=\sum_{k=t}^{T} r_k.
\]
A value model $V_\psi(s_t)$ predicts the expected return from state $s_t$, and the advantage used by PPO is
\[
  \hat A_t = G_t - V_\psi(s_t).
\]

\paragraph{Token-level advantages with GAE.}
To reduce variance, many implementations use generalized advantage estimation (GAE).
First define the temporal-difference residual
\[
  \delta_t = r_t + \gamma V_\psi(s_{t+1}) - V_\psi(s_t),
\]
and then compute the advantage as
\[
  \hat A_t =
  \sum_{l=0}^{T-t-1}
  (\gamma\lambda)^l
  \delta_{t+l}.
\]
The resulting token-level advantages $\hat A_t$ are then used in the PPO loss to update the policy.

Intuitively, the reward model provides a sequence-level signal, which is then converted into token-level advantages indicating whether each sampled action $y_t$ led to a better or worse outcome than expected. GAE typically produces lower-variance estimates and therefore more stable training.


So PPO in RLHF is usually actor-critic:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item actor: policy LM,
  \item critic: value head/model,
  \item reward model: separate learned preference scorer.
\end{enumerate}

\textbullet~ \textbf{Typical loop:} A typical loop is:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Sample prompts $\mx$.

  \item Generate responses $\my \sim \pi_{\theta_{\mathrm{old}}}(\cdot \mid \mx)$.

  \item Compute:
  \begin{itemize}
  \item log-probs from current/old policy,
  \item log-probs from reference model,
  \item reward model score,
  \item KL penalty,
  \item total reward.
  \end{itemize}

  \item Estimate advantages $\hat{A}_t$ and returns.

  \item Update:
  \begin{itemize}
  \item policy with PPO clipped objective,
  \item value model with regression loss,
  \item often also include entropy regularization.
  \end{itemize}

  \item Repeat.
\end{enumerate}

\textbullet~ \textbf{Drawbacks of PPO-based RLHF}.

PPO-based RLHF works, but it has real drawbacks:

\begin{enumerate} 
  \item \textbf{Expensive.}

  You must generate rollouts online and run:
  \begin{itemize}
  \item policy model,
  \item reference model,
  \item reward model,
  \item value model.
  \end{itemize}
  That is a lot of compute.

  \item \textbf{Sensitive / unstable.}

  Training can be delicate:
  \begin{itemize}
  \item KL coefficient $\beta$,
  \item clipping threshold $\epsilon$,
  \item reward scaling,
  \item value loss balance,
  \item rollout length.
  \end{itemize}
  These all matter.

  \item \textbf{Reward hacking.}

  The policy may exploit imperfections in the reward model.

  \item \textbf{Credit assignment is crude.}

  A single sequence-level preference score is often spread across many tokens, which is not always ideal.

  \item \textbf{Not always the best modern choice.}

  Many newer alignment methods avoid full PPO because it is heavy and complicated.
\end{enumerate}


\section{Megatron}

\textbullet~ Megatron-Core: the modular library of distributed-training components.

\textbullet~ Megatron-LM: the reference training framework built on top of Megatron Core with ready-made scripts and recipes.

Megatron exists to train Transformer models that are too large, too deep, or too sequence-heavy for a single GPU or even a naïve multi-GPU setup.
Megatron Core provides composable building blocks for Transformer training together with multiple parallelism strategies that can be combined across many GPUs.
The most important ones to understand first are data parallelism (DP), tensor parallelism (TP), pipeline parallelism (PP), context parallelism (CP), and expert parallelism (EP).

Typical usage:

\textbullet~ Forward TP:	 All-Gather

\textbullet~ Backward TP:	Reduce-Scatter

\textbullet~ Data Parallel Gradients:	All-Reduce

\paragraph{Two styles of pipeline parallelism}
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{GPipe style:} Do all forward microbatches first, then all backward.
  \item \textbf{1F1B (one-forward-one-backward):} After warmup, alternate forward and backward more tightly.
\end{enumerate}

Megatron commonly uses 1F1B-style scheduling, because it is more memory-efficient and practical at scale.

\paragraph{Context Parallelism}
With context parallelism, we split the sequence length across GPUs.
So instead of each rank storing the full sequence, each rank stores a chunk of the context.

Motivation: Long context is expensive because activations and attention memory grow strongly with sequence length.
Even when the model itself fits, the activation memory for long sequences may not fit.

CP is mainly a solution of long sequence training, activation memory pressure from sequence length and scaling to larger context windows.

The hidden states are partitioned along sequence dimension.
However, this introduces a problem for attention computation. 
In self-attention, each token can attend to many other tokens.
So CP requires communication to make attention work across partitioned sequence chunks.
This is due to that even though tokens are sharded, attention dependency is global over the context window.
So CP introduces communication around K/V or attention-related states so that each shard can attend correctly.

TP splits \textbf{features}. CP splits \textbf{tokens}.


\paragraph{Combined Parallelism}
One of the main strengths of Megatron is these strategies are not alternatives in a menu.
They are meant to be composed.

A typical world size decomposition looks like: 
\begin{verbatim}
  world size = TP x PP x DP.
\end{verbatim}


The role of ``parallel\_state'': The official API docs describe it as the place that manages ranks and groups for tensor, data, context, pipeline, and expert model parallelism.

Before training can begin, Megatron must answer questions like:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Which ranks are in my tensor-parallel group?
  \item Which ranks are in my pipeline stage group?
  \item Which ranks are data-parallel replicas of each other?
  \item If I am an MoE rank, what is my expert group?
  \item If context parallelism is enabled, who shares my sequence partition?
\end{enumerate}


\paragraph{Activation Checkpointing:}
During a normal forward pass, the model computes many intermediate tensors, called \boxtext{activations}, and keeps them in memory because the backward pass will need them to compute gradients.

With activation checkpointing, we do not save all of those intermediate activations.
Instead, we save only a small subset of them, called checkpoints.
Then, during backward, when some missing activation is needed, we recompute it by rerunning part of the forward pass.
So the tradeoff is between less GPU memory and more computation time.
This is mainly to reduce the amount of memory needed by activations, which can be huge if the model is deep, the sequence length is long or the batch size is large.
A simple example would be 
\begin{align*}
  x \mapsto f_1 \mapsto f_2 \mapsto f_3 \mapsto f_4 \mapsto y,
\end{align*}
Without checkpointing, we need to store $f_1(x), f_2, f_3,  f_4$, so backward can use them directly.
With gradient checkpointing, we may only store $x, f_2, y$, then during backward, if we need the output of $f_3$, we recompute it from the nearest saved point.
Basically we are replacing memory usage with recomputation.

In practice, once models become moderately large, people very often combine data / tensor / pipeline parallelism, mixed precision and activation checkpointing.

In practice, \boxtext{activation checkpointing} and \boxtext{gradient checkpointing} usually means the same thing.



\paragraph{Prelinminaries:}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \begin{lstlisting}
    parallel_state.initialize_model_parallel():
  \end{lstlisting}
  After initializing PyTorch distributed, Megatron Core is initialized with model-parallel settings, including tensor-model-parallel and pipeline-model-parallel sizes.
  It read the desired decomposition of the world, builds the process groups for TP, PP, DP, and possibly others and stores rank/group metadata so later modules know how to communicate.

  \item \begin{lstlisting}
    TransformerConfig():
  \end{lstlisting}
  This is the model-level object we instantiate for a GPT-style stack. In Megatron, the ``model'' is not just a plain PyTorch stack. 
  Its layers and execution are aware of the distributed topology. 
  So when we instantiate a Megatron model, we are not merely allocating tensors; we are instantiating a distributed computation structure.

  \item Forward Step functions: Megatron Core uses schedules.py to run the model. Define a forward step function that takes the \boxed{\text{data iterator}} and \boxed{\text{the model}} as input and produces the \boxed{\text{output}} tensor and a \boxed{\text{loss function}}.
  \begin{lstlisting}
    def forward_step_func(data_iterator, model):
      # pass 
      return output_tensor, partial(loss_func, loss_mask)  
  \end{lstlisting}
  The partial creates something behaving like:
  \begin{lstlisting}
    def reduced_loss_func(output_tensor):
      return loss_func(loss_mask, output_tensor)
  \end{lstlisting}

  \item Distributed Checkpoint: Megatron Core uses distributed checkpoints for loading and saving models.
  This allows you to convert the model from one parallel setting to another when you load it. For example, a model trained with tensor parallel size 2, can be loaded again as a tensor model with parallel size 4.
  \begin{lstlisting}
    def save_distributed_checkpoint(checkpoint_path, gpt_model):
      sharded_state_dict = gpt_model.sharded_state_dict(prefix='')
      dist_checkpointing.save(sharded_state_dict=sharded_state_dict, 
      checkpoint_dir=checkpoint_path)

  def load_distributed_checkpoint(checkpoint_path, gpt_model):
      sharded_state_dict=gpt_model.sharded_state_dict(prefix='')
      checkpoint = dist_checkpointing.load(sharded_state_dict=
      sharded_state_dict, checkpoint_dir=checkpoint_path)
      gpt_model.load_state_dict(checkpoint)
      return gpt_model
  \end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}


\paragraph{Context parallel package}
Context Parallelism (``CP'') is a parallelization scheme on the dimension of sequence length. Unlike prior SP (sequence parallelism) which only splits the sequence of Dropout and LayerNorm activations CP partitions the network inputs and all activations along sequence dimension.

With CP, all modules except attention (e.g., Linear, LayerNorm, etc.) can work as usual without any changes, because they do not have inter-token operations.

As for attention:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The $\mQ$ of each token needs to compute with the KV (key and value) of all tokens in the same sequence.
  Hence, CP requires additional all-gather across GPUs to collect the full sequence of $\mK, \mV$.
  Correspondingly, reduce-scatter should be applied to the activation gradients of $\mK, \mV$ in backward propagation.
  (Recall that in forward, every rank temporarily used the full $\mK, \mV$. In backward, gradients with respect to $\mK, \mV$ are produced from all those attention computations. However, each original $\mK, \mV$, chunk belongs to one owning rank, so the gradients must be accumulated and sent back to the correct owner.)

  A small example would be when computing attention forward, imagine we have 2 GPUs, we initially have 
  \begin{verbatim}
    GPU0: Q0, K0, V0 
    GPU1: Q1, K1, V1
  \end{verbatim}
  To compute the attention, we need to call all\_gather, after which the situation becomes 
  \begin{verbatim}
    GPU0: Q0, [K0, K1], [V0, V1] --> computing O0
    GPU1: Q1, [K0, K1], [V0, V1] --> computing O1
  \end{verbatim}
  When the backward happens, the loss gradient arriving from above is 
  \begin{verbatim}
    GPU0: d L / d O0
    GPU1: d L / d O1
  \end{verbatim}
  Right now, on GPU0, contributions to the gradient of $\mQ_0$, $\mK_0$, $\mV_0$, $\mK_1$, $\mV_1$ will be generated, we write the chain rule formula 
  \begin{align*}
    \frac{\partial L}{\partial \mQ_0} &= \frac{\partial L}{\partial \mO_0} \cdot \frac{\partial \mO_0}{\partial \mQ_0} \\
