    \frac{\partial L}{\partial \mK_0} &= \frac{\partial L}{\partial \mO_0}\frac{\partial \mO_0}{\partial \mK_0} + \frac{\partial L}{\partial \mO_1}\frac{\partial \mO_1}{\partial \mK_0}.
  \end{align*}
  The contribution of $\mQ_0$ in gradient can be easily computed, however, for $\mK_0$, it participates also the computation of $\mO_1$ on GPU1, which requires us to do first reduce (sum), and then scatter \footnote{These matrix products are really backprop shorthand, not strict elementary scalar chain rule notation.}.

  \item An important memory optimization: during forward, each rank stores only its local $\mK, \mV$ activations rather than permanently storing the full-sequence $\mK, \mV$. In backward, the needed $\mK, \mV$ are gathered again, which preserves the memory benefits of CP.
  
  \item CP is enabled by simply setting context\_parallel\_size=<CP\_SIZE> in command line.
  
  \item All-gather and reduce-scatter are implemented under the hood as point-to-point communication in a ring topology. This implementation is optimized.
  Logically, the model still needs the effect of all-gather and reduce-scatter. Physically, that communication can be carried out as staged ring exchanges for better efficiency.

  \item The docs explicitly point out that CP benefits from MQA and GQA because those attention variants reduce the number of key/value heads, which reduces the size of communicated $\mK,\mV$ tensors.
  
  \item \textbf{Why CP is useful for long context?} Long-context training often becomes memory-bound because activation memory grows with sequence length.
  Full activation recomputation can add around 30\% overhead, while simply increasing tensor parallelism can also become inefficient because communication becomes harder to hide. CP is presented as a better fit when the real bottleneck is sequence length.

  The underlying idea is simple: TP helps when layers are too large, CP helps when sequences are too long.
  With CP size $C$, each GPU owns about $1/C$ of the sequence, so activation memory per GPU drops accordingly, while the communication is focused mainly around attention rather than every part of the model.
  
\end{enumerate}

A somewhat more complicated example is the following: 2 TP + 2 CP, this is presented in the Nvidia's Megatron docs.
\begin{figure}[t]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{Figures/CP_overview.png}
  \caption{2 TP + 2 CP}
  \label{fig:cp_overview}
\end{figure}

TP groups: (GPU0, GPU1) for seq chunk 0, and (GPU2, GPU3) for seq chunk 1.
This means that GPU0 and GPU1 both work on the same local sequence chunk, but with different TP shards. GPU2 and GPU3 do the same for the other sequence chunk.
GPU0 exchanges context information with GPU2, GPU1 exchanges context information with GPU3.

Suppose the input hidden states are $\mX \in \R^{B \times S \times H}$, with CP$=2$, the sequence is split into two chunks:
\begin{align*}
  \mX^0 \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times H}, \quad \mX^1 \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times H}.
\end{align*}

\paragraph{The first yellow AG / RS: TP-side activation layout bookkeeping}
These yellow boxes are best understood as the TP-side communication/layout transitions that appear when Megatron combines TP with sequence-parallel-style activation handling.
To be specific, inside each TP group, Megatron is not storing the full $\mX^0$ on both TP ranks all the time.
To save activation memory, it keeps the activations sequence-sharded across the TP ranks, so for the top group, $\mX^0$ is split again along the sequence dimension:
\begin{align*}
  \mX^0 = \sbrac{\mX^{00}, \mX^{01}}, \text{   where  } \mX^{00}, \mX^{01} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{4} \times H}.
\end{align*}
We do all gather here so each GPU now has an full input for the corresponding chunk.

Now consider the QKV part, which we assume is a column parallel linear layer, the weight is splitted along the output dimension:
\begin{align*}
  \mW_A = \sbrac{\mW_A^0, \mW_A^1}, \quad A \in {Q, K, V}
\end{align*}
Here, we compute both $\mX^0\mW_A^0, \mX^0\mW_A^1$, which are both $\R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times \frac{H}{2}}$, which we will denote as $\mQ^{00}, \mQ^{01}$ respectively.

Now comes the blue AG, this is because each query token need key and value from all tokens in the same sequence.
After the blue AG, on GPU0, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \mQ^{00} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times \frac{H}{2}}, \quad [\mK^{00}, \mK^{10}] \in \R^{B \times S \times \frac{H}{2}}, \quad [\mV^{00}, \mV^{10}],
\end{align*}
similarly on GPU1, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \mQ^{01} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times \frac{H}{2}}, \quad [\mK^{01}, \mK^{11}] \in \R^{B \times S \times \frac{H}{2}}, \quad [\mV^{01}, \mV^{11}],
\end{align*}
Notice that the tensor parallelism is kept, and the attnetion is computed in the following Attn block, resulting in $\mO^{00}, \mO^{01} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times \frac{H}{2}}$ on GPU0, GPU1 respectively.

Then in the Attn out layer, we do projection. 
This is the part that is row-parallel.
RowParallelLinear means the weight is split along the input dimension, and the input is assumed to already be split across TP ranks.
The reason for doing so is obvious, if we notice that on each GPU, we keep the input matrix in the shape of $B \times \frac{S}{2} \times \frac{H}{2}$, we naturally need $\mW_O^{00}, \mW_O^{01} \in \R^{\frac{H}{2} \times H}$ 
So on GPU0, we have 
\begin{align*}
  \mP^{00} = \mO^{00} \times \mW_O^{00} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times H},
\end{align*}
and similar on GPU1 
\begin{align*}
  \mP^{01} = \mO^{01} \times \mW_O^{01} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times H}
\end{align*}
These are all partial sums, and the result is given by 
\begin{align*}
  \mY^0 = \mP^{00} + \mP^{01} \in \R^{B \times \frac{S}{2} \times H}.
\end{align*}
This is why another reduce-scatter is called here.

During the next reduce-scatter, we reduce to get $\mY^0$ and $\mY^1$ and reshard them along the sequence dimension.

Notice that the rest does not involve CP anymore because there is no inter token actions.


\end{document}
